                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202303812')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2d0249cbfb214385861f067ccc6df7d8">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '81750938-e965-4893-8c6d-2aaf7007bd34'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d122500892" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>P2-type layered oxide material Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is a competitive candidate for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Nevertheless, it suffers from the strong P2–O2 phase transition during charging to the high voltage regime, rendering drastic volume variations and poor cycling performance. Here, a Quasi-zero strain P2-Na<sub>0.75</sub>Li<sub>0.15</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode is synthesized, which reflects the vanishing P2–O2 transition with a volume change as low as 0.49%, thus resulting in the material an excellent cycling performance (83.9% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 5 C). The low-volume strain can be attributed to two aspects: (1) the Mg<sup>2+</sup> riveted in the Na layer can act as a “pillar” to stabilize the crystal structure under the condition of sodium removal, thus restricting the structural changes under high voltage. (2) The entry of Li<sup>+</sup> into the transition metal (TM) layer can mitigate the electron localization in the highly desodiation state and can effectively immobilize the coordination oxygen atoms, thus suppressing the slip of P2–O2 transition. This study not only provides a new insight of Li and Mg synergetic substitution effect on the structural stability of P2-type cathode, but also an efficient avenue for developing cathode materials of SIBs with ultralow bulk strain.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202303812-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted widespread attention for large-scale energy storage owing to the global abundance and low cost of sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0001_R_d122500884e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0003_R_d122500884e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, to enhance the energy density and working voltage of these batteries, the development of high-performance cathode materials is crucial.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0004_R_d122500884e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0005_R_d122500884e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recent studies on cathode materials have revealed the transition metal layered oxides (Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub>, TM: transition metal, Ti, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, etc.) exhibit tremendous potential for practical application due to their straightforward synthesis, excellent electrochemical performance and convenience of industrialization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0006_R_d122500884e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>,</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0007_R_d122500884e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Environ. Sci.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Of particular interest is P2 and O3 of Na-based layered oxides, where O and P correspond to the coordination environment of sodium ions in the alkali metal layer (O for octahedron, P for trigonal prismatic) and the numbers represent the minimum number of layers in the periodic sequence formed by the stacking of oxygen atoms (2 corresponds to ABBAABBA…, 3 corresponds to ABCABC…).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0008_R_d122500884e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Phys. B">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O3-phase cathodes typically exhibit higher specific capacity than P2-phase cathodes, which is attributed to the higher initial Na content.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0004_R_d122500884e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the awful diffusion capability of Na<sup>+</sup> in octahedral position point leadsto poor rate and cycling performance. Compared with the O3 phase, the P2 framework has higher ionic conductivity and lower diffusion barrier, which provides opportunities for excellent rate/cycle performance and stable structural properties, making it a highly promising cathode material for sodium ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0009_R_d122500884e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The typical P2 type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> could show an excellent specific capacity of 160&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with working voltage over 3.7&nbsp;V. However, when charging to 4.2&nbsp;V, there are fewer Na<sup>+</sup> in the Na layer structure and the electrostatic repulsion between two adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs increases, which will drive the transition metal layers to undergo relative glide to reduce the repulsive force of the system to achieve a stable structure, in which the so-called P2–O2 phase transition occurs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0010_R_d122500884e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This introduces drastic volume variations, leading to structural damage and poor stability of the material.</p>
         
         <p>Recent research demonstrates that substitution or doping of nonredox active elements such as Li<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, Al<sup>3+</sup>, and Ti<sup>4+</sup> is an efficient way to convert the P2 phase into the less hazardous OP4/Z or to inhibit its phase structure transformation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0011_R_d122500884e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0017_R_d122500884e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Sci.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Through extensive investigations, the way of Li and Mg substitution has attracted our attention. By substituting monovalent Li<sup>+</sup> in the material, more Na<sup>+</sup> can be accommodated in the initial P2 phase, leading to an improvement in the theoretical specific capacity. Additionally, the substitution of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/<sup>3+</sup> by Li<sup>+</sup> results in an increase in the concentration of Mn<sup>4+</sup> in the material. This, in turn, reduces the Jahn–Teller distortion and suppresses the P2–O2 slip while adhering to the principle of charge conservation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0018_R_d122500884e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0019_R_d122500884e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Power Sources">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In a study conducted by Zhao et&nbsp;al., they prepared P2-Na<sub>45/54</sub>Li<sub>4/54</sub>Ni<sub>16/54</sub>Mn<sub>34/54</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with a high Na content and found that the addition of Li promoted the oxidation of Ni<sup>2+</sup>to the high-valent state even at a low cut-off voltage (below 4&nbsp;V).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0020_R_d122500884e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the higher Na content allows the material to maintain the P2 phase even when charged to 4.6&nbsp;V, ensuring a high structural stability. Apart from Li substitution, some researchers found that Mg<sup>2+</sup> substitution is able to enhance the structural properties of layered materials and suppress the P2–O2 transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0021_R_d122500884e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a>-<a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0023_R_d122500884e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Kubota et&nbsp;al. studied comparatively the structural changes of Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Al<sup>3+</sup> doped P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials during charging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0024_R_d122500884e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They found that the Al-doped samples still had a large amount of P2–O2 phase transition, while Mg doping was able to suppress this unfavorable trend. The authors considered that Mg<sup>2+</sup> carries a relatively low charge number compared to Al<sup>3+</sup>, which would locally form a [Mg<sup>2+</sup>O<sub>6</sub>] polyhedron with a relatively high negative charge number, generating a stronger electrostatic attraction to the neighboring sodium ions in the alkali layer, and the residual sodium ions could play a role in stabilizing the structure. Moreover, the synergistic substitution of Li and Mg has been explored, Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Li<sub>1/7</sub>Mn<sub>5/14</sub>][Mg<sub>1/7</sub>Mn<sub>5/14</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.73</sub>Li<sub>0.11</sub>Mg<sub>0.12</sub>Mn<sub>0.77</sub>O<sub>2</sub> have been demonstrated to simultaneously realize high activity and reversibility of lattice O redox.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0025_R_d122500884e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0026_R_d122500884e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Energy">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> P2/P3-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Li<sub>0.06</sub>Mg<sub>0.06</sub>Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> has been designed to integrate the benefits of the P2 and P3 phase, exhibiting a highly reversible P2/P3-OP4/P3 phase transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0027_R_d122500884e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Energy">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Chen et&nbsp;al. prepared Na<sub>0.67</sub>(Li<sub>0.5</sub>Mg<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>0.1</sub>(Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>)<sub>0.9</sub>O<sub>2</sub> to explain the origin of the enhanced electrochemical properties of Li/Mg co-substituted P2 layered oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0028_R_d122500884e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chin. J. Struct. Chem">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although these works provide valuable insights for understanding structure changes during the charge/discharge process and have reported many materials without P2–O2 phase transition, most of the reported work, to our knowledge, still ignores the adverse effects caused by the accumulation in terms of volume strain during long cycles in those no phase transition systems.</p>
         
         <p>Motivated by this, the development of a cathode material with an ultra-low volume change (&lt;1%) is crucial, and it should utilize the benefits of both Li and Mg elements to mitigate the negative consequences resulting from volume changes during the charging and discharging processes. Herein, we have successfully prepared P2-Na<sub>0.75</sub>Li<sub>0.15</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LMNM) by Li and Mg synergetic substitution. Scanning transmission electron microscopy annular dark field (STEM–ADF) and density functional theory (DFT) calculations determine that Mg<sup>2+</sup> were entering the Na layer rather than the TM layer, which are capable of serving as the pillar of the material. While Li<sup>+</sup> enter the TM layer could effectively stabilized the coordination oxygen atoms. As a result, LMNM cathode not only suppress the slip caused by the repulsive force between O–O of adjacent transition metal layers, thus prevent harmful P2–O2 transition during charge/discharge process, but also achieved an excellent electrochemical performance including high reversible specific capacity and outstanding cycling retention. More importantly, in situ X–ray diffraction (XRD) result shows that the material has an ultralow volume change (0.49%) when charged to 4.35&nbsp;V, much smaller than P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (23%). Last but not least, LMNM demonstrates excellent air stability, as evidenced by the absence of any structural changes or capacity decay after 30 days of aging in air.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202303812-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202303812-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural Characteristics and Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>A series of P2-type materials with elevated Na content (<i>x</i> &gt; 0.67) Na<sub>0.75</sub>Li<sub>0.15</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LMNM), Na<sub>0.75</sub>Li<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LNM), Na<sub>0.75</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (MNM), and Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NM) were designed pursuing the criterion of cationic potential defined by Zhao et&nbsp;al. (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0029_R_d122500884e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Science">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The four samples were synthesized via a simple solid-state reaction method, and the synthesis procedure and experimental details are provided in the Supporting Information (Materials Synthesis in the Experimental Section). We first investigated the electrochemical performance of the four compounds using Na half-cells in the voltage range of 2–4.35&nbsp;V. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves under the first three cycles with a sweep rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. There are five pairs of distinct redox peaks (3.25/2.99, 3.41/3.27, 3.65/3.5, 3.74/3.62, and 4.23/3.98&nbsp;V) in the CV curve of NM, corresponding to the five voltage plateaus in the galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curve. The first four pairs of redox peaks correspond to the Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> process, accompanied by the Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0030_R_d122500884e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0031_R_d122500884e750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specially, the fifth pair of peaks around 4.2&nbsp;V becomes shorter and shifts to lower potential from the 1st to 3rd cycle, indicating that the structural changes are irreversible which corresponds to the P2–O2 phase transition. For Mg-substituted MNM samples, the redox peaks (3.24/2.94, 3.43/3.30, 3.63/3.56, and 4.30/4.01&nbsp;V) in the CV curves become shorter and broader compared to NM. In addition, the reversibility of the redox peaks around at 4.2&nbsp;V increases significantly, indicating that the substitution of Mg<sup>2+</sup> enhances the stability of the material. Unlike the NM and MNM samples, there are only two redox peaks at 3.15/2.90&nbsp;V remain for the LNM sample, whereas the oxidation peak above 4&nbsp;V is right-shifted and exhibited incomplete emergence, corresponding to the disappearance of the plateau into a slope in the GCD curves, indicating the suppressed Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering and the P2–O2 phase transition, but the peak above 4&nbsp;V continuously decreases with cycling. The LMNM sample combines the advantages of MNM and LNM, The redox peak position (3.15/2.91&nbsp;V) is similar to that of LNM. It can be seen that the CV curves of the first week are slightly different from those of the two subsequent circles, but the high overlap of the subsequent CV curves suggests that the Na<sup>+</sup> embedding/detachment process is well reversible.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303812-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/26783cb0-baa4-4dd2-a199-afadae3ec97d/adfm202303812-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/26783cb0-baa4-4dd2-a199-afadae3ec97d/adfm202303812-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/348e7448-b721-4b4d-954f-d42d7f7644cb/adfm202303812-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/26783cb0-baa4-4dd2-a199-afadae3ec97d/adfm202303812-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202303812-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202303812" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance: a) CV curves at 0.1 mv s<sup>−1</sup> and b) GCD curves of four samples at 0.1 C (1 C = 100&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) between 2 and 4.35&nbsp;V. c) Rate capability, d) Cycling performance at 1 C of four cathode materials. e) Variation of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> as functions of potential (V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) determined by GITT.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0001">1b</a> shows GCD curves of the first three cycles for the four materials at a current density of 0.1 C (1 C = 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). It is worth noting that NM could achieve a high initial discharge capacity of 140.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, but decays rapidly to 110.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> by the third cycle, with a stepped electrochemical curve and a longer voltage plateau above 4.2&nbsp;V where the P2–O2 phase transition happens. Thus, the phase transition is inferred to play a vital role in the capacity loss of NM. Different from the NM, the specific capacity of MNM maintains well with the cycle number increase. The first and the third cycle discharge capacities of MNM were 138.8 and 135.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Such a low capacity loss of MNM indicates that the substitution of Mg is instrumental in stabilizing the material structure. For the LNM doped with Li alone, the discharge capacity is lower (only 125.7&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) in the first week comparing to that of NM and MNM. However, with the electrochemical activation process, the discharge capacity LNM could finally stabilize at 130&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the third cycle instead. Interestingly, the electrochemical curve is smooth without any voltage plateau above 4.2&nbsp;V. Instead, it starts at 4.1&nbsp;V with the “slope area”, which implies that the material switches from a high-voltage phase transition to a solid-solution reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0032_R_d122500884e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0033_R_d122500884e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with the previous three materials, the LMNM exhibits a smoother GCD curve. The “ramp region” is observed to start at 4&nbsp;V in the first week and disappears completely in the second cycle, showing the solid solution behavior throughout the voltage interval.</p>
            
            <p>The rate performance of LMNM, LNM, MNM, and NM are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0001">1c</a>. Although MNM and NM have higher initial capacities, the capacity advantage of LMNM starts to emerge from 0.5 C. Even at 5 C, the LMNM provides a reversible capacity of up to 86.6&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current returns to 0.2 C, the reversible capacity of LMNM achieves 130&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, slightly exceeding that of initial capacity, which is attributed to the electrochemical activation including electrolyte attains full wetting and additional active sites exposure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0034_R_d122500884e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>-<a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0036_R_d122500884e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The excellent rate performance of LMNM is related to its solid solution behavior in the full voltage interval, which facilitates the rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0001">1d</a> shows the cycling performance of the four materials at a current of 1 C for 150 cycles, with capacity retention of 90.5%, 80.1%, 59.1%, and 64.3% for LMNM, LNM, MNM, and NM, respectively. In addition, the capacity retention rate of LMNM is 98.5% after 100 cycles (0.5 C, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). And the capacity retention after 500 cycles at 5 C could even reach 83.9% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The excellent cycling and rate performance of LMNM indicates that Li and Mg synergetic substitution can improve the structural stability of the material as well as enhance the fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport ability. This result is also in a leading position in comparison with some of the reported electrochemical properties of Ni–Mn based layered oxides (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>To further understand the superior rate capability of LMNM, the kinetics of the electrode process were explored by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). The GITT curves of the four different materials during the second charge and the calculated sodium ion diffusion coefficients are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0001">1e</a>, respectively. The calculation details are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a> in the Supporting Information. (LMNM sample is selected as an example). As can be observed that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of LMNM is in the range of 10<sup>−9</sup>-10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than most Ni-Mn-based P2-type layered oxide cathodes (≈10<sup>−14</sup> to 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) and higher than all three materials including LNM, MNM, and NM, indicating that the synergetic substitution of Li and Mg improves the ionic conductivity of the materials and enhanced rate properties.</p>
            
            <p>In order to clarify the substitution position of Li and Mg, structural characterization was carried out to gain a more thorough understanding of the material's structure. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0002">2a</a> is the XRD patterns of four samples, where all peaks can be indexed to the P2 phase structure, shedding light on Li and Mg ions are uniformly introduced into the lattice. Moreover, the superlattice peak at 22.5° can be observed for LMNM and LNM from the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0001">1a</a>, which originated from the ordered Li/Mn distribution in TM layers with a <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/09bc78a2-26ac-45d1-b88e-ee5cab53b32f/adfm202303812-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202303812:adfm202303812-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202303812-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mrow>
                        <msqrt>
                           <mn>3</mn>
                        </msqrt>
                        <mi>a</mi>
                        <mo>×</mo>
                        <msqrt>
                           <mn>3</mn>
                        </msqrt>
                        <mi>a</mi>
                     </mrow>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\sqrt 3 a \times \sqrt 3 a$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>-type supercell (“1/3 1/3 1” superlattice).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0022_R_d122500884e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0037_R_d122500884e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0038_R_d122500884e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nature">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is well known that the Mg ions located in the TM layer can inhibit the superstructures, demonstrating that the Mg ions introduced into LMNM sample do not replace Ni or Mn ions, but instead enters the Na layer. DFT calculations were used to further illustrate the stability of the Mg<sup>2+</sup> sites, we establish two structural models with Mg<sup>2+</sup> located in the Na and TM layers, and calculate the formation energy of the two models. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0002">2b</a>, the Mg<sup>2+</sup> favors to intercalate into the Na layer with the more negative formation energy of −6.20&nbsp;eV, not the TM layers. With the same procedure, we also calculated the formation energy of Li<sup>+</sup> at different positions, and found that Li<sup>+</sup> is actually more inclined to occupy TM sites (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The details about the crystal structure of the compounds can be further determined from the Rietveld refinement of XRD patterns (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The crystal structures and refinement data as well as the atomic positions and occupation parameters of LMNM, LNM, MNM, and NM are listed in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3–S6</a>, respectively. The refinement results show that all compounds are single-phase P2-type crystal structures. For the structure of LMNM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0002">2c</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), Li<sup>+</sup> is successfully introduced into the TM layer and occupies the octahedral 2<i>c</i> Wyckoff sites with Ni, while Mg<sup>2+</sup> occupies the 6&nbsp;<i>g</i> Wyckoff of Na sites.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303812-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a5913e85-e6ad-46b0-8030-8d157c9f81f4/adfm202303812-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a5913e85-e6ad-46b0-8030-8d157c9f81f4/adfm202303812-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a1d289c5-8e2c-4978-8490-cc8b6590693d/adfm202303812-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a5913e85-e6ad-46b0-8030-8d157c9f81f4/adfm202303812-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202303812-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202303812" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of four samples. Inset is enlargement in the 2<i>θ</i> range of 18°–24°. b) Two Mg-doped crystal structure models with the corresponding formation energy. c) Rietveld refinement result of LMNM. d) SEM image and e) HRTEM image of LMNM, f) STEM-ADF image of the LMNM cathode material, g) SEM-EDS mapping of LMNM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image reveal that the LMNM exists in the form of 2–4&nbsp;µm lamellar particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0002">2d</a>). The high–resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) was used to further explore the microstructure of LMNM, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0002">2e</a>. The distance between adjacent lattice stripes in the HRTEM image is 5.56 Å, which coincides with the (002) crystal plane spacing calculated by the Bragg equation. To further identify the location of Mg<sup>2+</sup>, STEM-ADF technique is being used to obtain a mass-contrast image of the material with atomic resolution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0002">2f</a>). The well-preserved layered structure in the material indicates that the material has a high degree of crystallinity, but some isolated bright spots (marked by red boxes) are seen in the Na layers, suggesting the presence of heavier heteroatoms in these layers instead of Na atoms. Considering the lining degree of these bright spots are less than the Ni and Mn atoms in the TM layer, we think Mg ions are probably located in the Na layer, which is consistent with the conclusion from DFT calculation above. In addition, energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping shows that the elements Na, Mg, Ni, Mn, and O were uniformly distributed throughout the particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0002">2g</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202303812-sec-0040-title">2.2 Mechanism of Structural Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>To monitor the structural evolution of the material LMNM during charging and discharging, the in situ XRD tests were performed as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0003">3a,b</a>. When charging up to 4.0&nbsp;V, we observed the (002) and (004) diffraction peaks move continuously to lower angles, indicating that interlayer spacing will increase with the detachment of Na ions. Meanwhile, the (010) and (012) diffraction peaks move continuously to higher angles, showing the a-b plane will contract during charging process. Notably, the peaks shift process of (002) and (004) will be significantly less shifted at the high voltage interval (4.0–4.35&nbsp;V), which indicates the involvement of the oxygen redox reaction, i.e., when the O<sup>2−</sup>/(O<sub>2</sub>)<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>−</sup> pair is involved in charge compensation, the increase in oxygen valence decreases the interlayer repulsion, which balances with the increase in interlayer repulsion due to Na ion delocalization, and the increment in the c-axis parameter is reduced.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0017_R_d122500884e1012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Sci.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even at the end of charging, the crystallinity of the material does not decrease significantly and remains in a perfect P2 phase structure. The redox reaction of lattice oxygen is related to oxygen lone-pair, the introduction of Li and Mg doping can generate Li–O–Na and Mg–O–Li coordination, thereby triggering the redox reaction of lattice oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0039_R_d122500884e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nat. Chem.">39</a>-<a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0041_R_d122500884e1017" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Joule">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This was also confirmed by ex situ X–ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), it can be obtained that the anion oxidation reaction occurs above 4&nbsp;V and remains in the subsequent cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303812-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/63c9a16f-863a-4655-90cc-a8331ae50b0f/adfm202303812-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/63c9a16f-863a-4655-90cc-a8331ae50b0f/adfm202303812-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e4779cd6-764a-4e4e-aa34-2b23cff1c3a5/adfm202303812-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/63c9a16f-863a-4655-90cc-a8331ae50b0f/adfm202303812-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202303812-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202303812" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure evolution: a) In situ XRD patterns of LMNM during the first cycle at 2–4.35&nbsp;V (0.2 C). b) Contour plots of (002) and (012) diffraction peaks. c) Evolution of lattice parameters during cycling. d) Comparison of LMNM first charge volume strain with Ni–Mn-based materials in other reported literatures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>All diffraction peaks of LMNM could shift back to the initial state after discharging, showing the excellent structural reversibility. The lattice parameters and cell volume changes were subsequently evaluated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0003">3c</a>. It can be observed that the cell volume decreases during charging process and the discharging process is opposite, which is a typical cell volume “breathing” behavior of layer structured cathode materials during cycling. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0003">3d</a>, the volume change rate of LMNM is at a much lower level (0.49%) compared to the Ni–Mn cathode materials reported in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0015_R_d122500884e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0017_R_d122500884e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Sci.">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0031_R_d122500884e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0032_R_d122500884e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0042_R_d122500884e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">42</a>-<a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0045_R_d122500884e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Energy Environ. Sci.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a sharp contrast, we also performed in situ XRD characterization of NM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The results show that the characteristic peak of O2 phase starts to appear in NM material when it is charged to 4.2&nbsp;V. The calculated volume change of the phase change fraction reaches 22.2%. Unlike the P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, NM samples contain a small amount of O2 phase and a large amount of P2 phase in the high-voltage plateau region, which may be related to the higher initial Na content of NM. To reveal the contribution of volume change behavior on the cycling performance, the XRD patterns of the electrodes between the initial state and after 100 cycles were further compared. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), the material is still perfectly indexed to the P2 phase structure after 100 cycles. The XRD refinement results show that the volume change of the LMNM material was 1.01% after multiple desodiation/sodiation and the rate of change was only 0.01% per cycle. In summary, a narrower volume changes means less strain accumulation, which provides a pleasant advantage for long-term cycling stability.</p>
            
            <p>For the purpose of investigating the role of Li<sup>+</sup> and Mg<sup>2+</sup> in its structural evolution, ex situ XRD tests were performed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting information). The (002) diffraction peak of LMNM is shifted to the left during the charging process, indicating that the <i>c</i>-axis spacing gradually increases, which is consistent with the in situ XRD results. It is noteworthy that the remaining Na<sup>+</sup> content in the system is about 0.28 at 4.35&nbsp;V (Supporting Information for calculation details), but still maintains a perfect P2 phase structure. On the contrary, the hydrate phase of NM appeared at 3.9&nbsp;V, but there are still 0.43 Na<sup>+</sup> remaining in the structure. After continually charging to 4.35&nbsp;V, the characteristic peak of P2 phase was extremely weak and almost completely transformed into hydrate phase. For the MNM sample, it showed a perfect P2 phase structure when charged to 3.9&nbsp;V. But the remaining Na<sup>+</sup> content in the system was calculated to be about 0.45, which is closer to the NM system. It is considered that the introduction of Mg<sup>2+</sup> into the Na layer can act as a “interlayer residual ion”, which produces electrostatic cohesion forces to adjacent TM layers to enhance the structural stability to a certain extent as a pillar. However, a large amount of Na<sup>+</sup> is de-embedded from system when charging to 4.35&nbsp;V and the hydrate phase could be indexed in the XRD pattern. The structural evolution of the material under study is not solely dependent on the Na<sup>+</sup> content in the system, as illustrated by the results based on the observations. In the case of LNM, the P2 phase is maintained during the charging process without the generation of hydrate phase. Combining literature research and experimental results,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0042_R_d122500884e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Engl.">42</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0046_R_d122500884e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nat. Commun.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> we speculate that Li<sup>+</sup> in the TM layer may have a regulatory effect on the distribution of Na<sup>+</sup>, so that it can resist the erosion of water molecules under the high sodium removal state, which can allow a high desodiation state to maintain the stability of the structure together with Mg “pillar”.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202303812-sec-0050-title">2.3 DFT Calculation</h3>
            
            <p>DFT calculations were carried out to further investigate the distribution state of Na<sup>+</sup>. The model diagram of the crystal structure of discharge and charge state is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> in the Supporting Information. The calculated formation energy <i>E</i><sub>f</sub> of single and double Na atoms aggregated near Ni atoms in Ni–Mn layered oxides are −3.04 and −6.37&nbsp;eV, respectively, while they are −2.87 and −6.14&nbsp;eV near Mn atoms. The lower formation energy of Na atoms makes the unembedded Na atoms more prone to aggregate in the vicinity of Ni atoms during the charging process. This aggregation phenomenon could also be understood from electronic structure calculation. As shown in the partial density of states (PDOS) results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0004">4b</a>), pure NM shows a semiconductor behavior with the valence band maximum (VBM) mainly contributed by Mn 3d and O 2p orbitals, while the conduction band minimum (CBM) is mainly contributed by Ni 3d and O 2p orbitals. After Na atoms intercalation, the electron transfer from Na will partially occupy the CBM, then push the Ni 3d and O 2p orbital in CBM under the Fermi level. This reveals that the Ni and O redox couples are the main electron acceptor from Na atoms. Comparing to Na atoms near Mn or Ni atoms, the NM system has single (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0004">4b</a>) and double (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) Na atoms exhibiting similar electronic structures, indicating that the electron transfer from Na favors to occupy the Ni 3d band states no matter where the Na ion is. The additional differential charge density diagrams depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0004">4c,d</a> (the 3D model structure with the slice sketch map was shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> in the Supporting Information) illustrate that in the presence of Na atoms in the interlayer spacing of the layered transition metal oxide, it facilitates the accumulation of electron near other atoms (Mn, Ni, O) in close proximity. The presence of Ni atoms exacerbates this aggregation phenomenon, as the charge is more likely to transfer to the Ni and O atoms in its vicinity. This is consistent with the Bader charge analysis (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), where the average valence state of Mn atoms remains essentially unchanged as the number of interlayer Na atoms increases, but the valence state of Ni atoms decreases noticeably. As a result, under the influence of the electronic structure, Na atoms tend to move spontaneously towards Ni atoms, resulting in an uneven distribution of Na in the NM system. This may be another major reason for the P2–O2 transition in the NM system during the charging process, and not solely due to the remaining Na content. However, this situation is improved in the LMNM system.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303812-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/501ec684-cd85-40ef-b02e-e66cb5399dce/adfm202303812-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/501ec684-cd85-40ef-b02e-e66cb5399dce/adfm202303812-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f81fc9dc-5a17-4380-81a8-87904bce5103/adfm202303812-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/501ec684-cd85-40ef-b02e-e66cb5399dce/adfm202303812-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202303812-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202303812" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) LMNM model structure of discharge and charge state. b) Projected density of states (PDOS) of O-2p, Mn-3d, and Ni-3d orbitals for NM system with and without Na atom. c,d) Differential charge density diagrams for NM system with one and two Na atoms near the Mn atoms (c) and Ni atoms (d). e,f) Electronic localization function map of NM system (e) and LMNM system (f).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To gain deeper insights into the atomic-level changes that occur in layered transition metal oxides during the charge–discharge process, systematic DFT calculations were performed using various models. Following structural optimization, the cell volume of LMNM was found to be 739.15 Å<sup>3</sup> in the discharged state and 727.52 Å<sup>3</sup> in the charged state, resulting in a volume change rate of 1.57%, which is in agreement with the experimental evaluation (0.49%). Meanwhile, the calculated volume changes of NM system without phase change could reach 6.25%. The parameters of optimized supercells are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> in the Supporting Information. This result embodies that the Li, Mg synergetic substitution do have positive effect on volume maintenance of structure. To further understand its reason, the electronic localization function (ELF) analysis was conducted as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0004">4e,f</a> (the 3D model structure with the slice sketch map was shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> in the Supporting Information). It can be seen that for the discharge state, the electron localization in the TM layer of the LMNM and NM systems is not significant when a sufficient number of Na atoms are uniformly embedded in the interlayer. After the charging process, however, the Na ions are released, resulting in a few Na atoms remaining between the layers and enhanced electron localization among the transition metal layer atoms, implying a stronger covalent bonding interaction. Stronger covalent bonds lead to shorter bond lengths, which means that the O atomic tends to converge toward the transition metals, leading to a considerable reduction in the cell parameters in the <i>a</i>- and <i>b</i>-axis directions. These microscopic results are manifested macroscopically as the volume of the electrode material becomes smaller during the charging process. The comparison shows that the electron localization phenomenon of the NM system is significantly stronger than that of the LMNM system in the charged state. Therefore, the electrode materials of the LMNM system can effectively mitigate the enhancement of electron localization after desodiating, and could effectively stabilize the coordination oxygen atoms, thus suppressing the slip of P2–O2 transition.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202303812-sec-0060-title">2.4 Analysis of Application Prospect</h3>
            
            <p>As we all know, air-sensitive P2-type oxide can develop cracks and hydration phases when exposed to humidity air, affecting the life of materials and making storage more difficult.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0046_R_d122500884e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nat. Commun.">46</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0047_R_d122500884e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Eng. J.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, there is no doubt that air stability is also an issue that cannot be ignored. For this purpose, the air stability of LMNM was investigated with aging experiments. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0005">5a</a> shows the original and aged XRD spectra of LMNM, it can be seen that LMNM also shows excellent structural stability after water and air treatment because the characteristic peaks can be perfectly matched. Next, we performed electrochemical performance tests on the aged material, and the results showed that the capacity and cycle life of the air- and water-treated samples were not significantly reduced (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). These all confirm the excellent air stability of LMNM sample. Based on the excellent structural characteristics of LMNM cathode and its enhanced performance in half-cells, a full cell was constructed to evaluate its feasibility for practical applications. The half-cell performance of the hard carbon anode was first investigated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0005">5c</a>), and a stable reversible capacity of ≈320&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained in the voltage range of 0.01–3&nbsp;V. A reasonable coupling of the P2-LMNM cathode to the hard carbon anode was achieved by adjusting the capacity ratio of the cathode to the anode to 1.05:1 and the voltage window to 1–4.35&nbsp;V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0005">5d</a> shows the charge/discharge profiles of the full battery at 0.1 C, an initial discharge capacity of 110&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the cathode active mass) with an average working voltage of ≈3.2&nbsp;V is obtained, enabling an admirable energy density of 310&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the total mass of cathode and anode active materials. Moreover, the full cell displays a respectable cycling life with a capacity retention of 82.9% at 0.5 C after 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202303812-fig-0005">5e</a>), which implies a large potential for practical applications. The thermal stability of the LMNM cathode material was assessed through DSC testing. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), the thermal decomposition temperature of the LMNM cathode in the highly desodiated-state was marginally reduced, indicating its favorable thermal stability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202303812-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/18e942ff-8fdf-4a51-ad5e-cfb70ad2ddbb/adfm202303812-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/18e942ff-8fdf-4a51-ad5e-cfb70ad2ddbb/adfm202303812-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d95d01bc-07f8-4a3b-8215-86157a65b978/adfm202303812-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/18e942ff-8fdf-4a51-ad5e-cfb70ad2ddbb/adfm202303812-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202303812-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202303812" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD spectra of LMNM pristine samples and those treated in water and air. b) Schematic of full cell (LMNM || HC). c) GCD curves of hard carbon (HC). d) GCD curves and e) cycling performance of full cell in the range of 1–4.35&nbsp;V.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202303812-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, we have explored a quasi-zero strain P2-type cathode material. As a verification, Li and Mg cosubstitute P2-LMNM have been successfully prepared. The in situ XRD results show a superior volume expansion of only 0.49% with no irreversible P2–O2 phase transition during the charge/discharge process. By ex situ XRD characterization and DFT calculations, Li and Mg synergetic substitution will regulate the Na layer “interlayer residual ion” content and make an uniform distribution state of Na<sup>+</sup> in the layers, which suppresses drastic structural change during desodiating and keep the material in P2 phase. ELF analysis further shows that the electrode materials of the LMNM system can effectively mitigate the enhancement of electron localization after desodiating, and could effectively stabilize the coordination oxygen atoms, thus suppressing the slip of P2–O2 transition and realizing the ultralow volume change level. Altogether, this study provides a new strategy to realize high capacity and low volume strain for SIB cathode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202303812-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202303812-sec-0110-title"> Materials Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>P2-Na<sub>0.75</sub>Li<sub>0.15</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was synthesized via a simple solid-phase reaction method. The stoichiometric ratios of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (9.66&nbsp;mmol, ≥99.8%, SCR, 3&nbsp;mol% excess), LiOH–H<sub>2</sub>O(3.86&nbsp;mmol, ≥95%, SCR, 3&nbsp;mol% excess), MgO (1.25&nbsp;mmol, 98%, Aladdin), NiO (2.50&nbsp;mmol, 99%, Aladdin), and MnO<sub>2</sub> (17.50&nbsp;mmol, 99%, Aladdin) were mixed in a planetary ball mill (QM-3SP04) at 400&nbsp;rpm for 5&nbsp;h in the presence of a small amount of acetone. The mixed materials were pressed into pellets under 10 Mpa. The pellets were heated to 900&nbsp;°C in a muffle furnace at 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> for 15&nbsp;h. Then, it was cooled to 200&nbsp;°C naturally and stored in a glovebox filled with argon gas. The control samples (LNM, MNM, NM) were prepared by a method akin to the experimental samples.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202303812-sec-0120-title"> Materials Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The XRD profile information of all samples was characterized by the PANalytical X'Pert 3 instrument with Cu <i>Kα</i> radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.5418 Å) in the scan range of 10°–90°. Rietveld refinement of XRD patterns was performed by Fullprof software. The morphology and microstructure of the materials were observed by scanning electron microscopy (Hitachi UHR FE-SEM SU8200 instrument) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEM-F200). Scanning transmission electron microscopy annular dark field (STEM-ADF) images were captured by a problem corrected STEM (Hitachi HF5000) working at 200&nbsp;kV. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed on the Thermo Scientific K-Alpha+ instrument to indicate the charge compensation of the cathode during the charge/discharge process. In-situ XRD testing was carried out using a homemade in-situ cell equipped with a beryllium window. Scanning in steps of 0.02° in the range of 10°–50°. The charge and discharge tests were performed at a current density of 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Electrodes with different charging depths were separated in an argon-filled glove box as samples for ex situ XRD and dried at 50&nbsp;°C for 3&nbsp;h after cleaning with diethyl carbonate DMC. The thermal stability was assessed through differential scanning calorimetry (DSC, Netzsch STA449) testing. The desodiated cathode was collected from coin cells charged to 4.35&nbsp;V.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202303812-sec-0130-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The prepared positive material was mixed with Super-P and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) at a mass ratio of 80:15:5. Then the well-mixed slurry was evenly coated on the aluminum foil and dried under vacuum at 100&nbsp;°C for 10&nbsp;h. The mass loading of the slice was at 3–4&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The CR2032 coin-type cells were assembled in an argon glove box (Mikrouna Super 1220/750/900) with water and oxygen content of less than 0.1&nbsp;ppm. The working electrodes were used as the cathode, the sodium foil as the anode, and the glass fiber membrane GF/F as the separator in between, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate (PC) with the additive of 5 vol% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte. The galvanostatic charge/discharge and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were performed on a Land battery test system instrument (Wuhan, China) at 30&nbsp;°C with a voltage range of 2–4.35&nbsp;V. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were executed employing a CHI660E electrochemical workstation (Shanghai Chenhua, China).</p>
                  
                  <p>We used 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate (PC) with the additive of 5 vol% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte to build full cells. Before building the full cell, the hard carbon was first precirculated in the half-cell for 5 cycles, and then removed from the cell at a discharge of 20&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The CR2032 coin-type full cells were assembled with hard carbon as the anode and LMNM as the cathode, the electrolyte dosage was 140&nbsp;µL. The positive and negative capacity ratio of full cells was set to 1.05:1 and the voltage window was set to 1–4.35&nbsp;V.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202303812-sec-0140-title"> Method of Calculation</h4>
                  
                  <p>Structure Model—In order to illustrate the formulation energy (<i>E</i><sub>f</sub>) of Li/Mg atoms in different layers and the accumulation of Na atoms near the Ni atoms, a Ni–Mn structure containing 36 O atoms, 16 Mn atoms, 2 Ni atoms, and 1–2 Li/Mg/Na atoms is constituted. For purpose of simulating the volume change during the charge–discharge process, the NM system's initial-state Na<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were modeled by 15 Na atoms, 6 Ni atoms, 14 Mn atoms and 40 O atoms, the LMNM system's initial-state Na<sub>0.75</sub>Li<sub>0.15</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were modeled by 15 Na atoms, 3 Li atoms, 1 Mg atoms, 3 Ni atoms, 14 Mn atoms, and 40 O atoms. Furthermore, 12 and 10 Na atoms were extracted from the initial states of the NM and LMNM systems, respectively, to model their desodiated-states.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202303812-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202303812-sec-0150-title"> DFT Calculation</h4>
                  
                  <p>All first-principle calculations based on density functional theory (DFT) were carried out within the projector augmented wave (PAW) method,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0048_R_d122500884e1388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Phys. Rev. B">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as implemented in the Vienna Ab Initio Simulation Package (VASP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0049_R_d122500884e1393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Rev. B">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The generalized gradient approximation (GGA) with the function of Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) was adopted to describe the exchange-correlation function.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0050_R_d122500884e1398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The convergence tolerance for the total energy and residual Hellmann–Feynman force were set to 10<sup>−4</sup>&nbsp;eV per formula unit and 0.02&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The cut-off energy was set to 400&nbsp;eV for the plane wave expansion and 9×9×3 <i>k</i>-point mesh was used for the integration in the irreducible Brillouin zone. For all calculations, Van der Waals Correction based on DFT-D and spin polarized were considered.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0051_R_d122500884e1410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Chem. Phys.">51</a>, <a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0052_R_d122500884e1410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Comput. Chem.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The DFT+U method was adopted to treat localized 3d electrons of Ni and Mn atoms, <i>U</i><sub>eff</sub> value was set to 3.1&nbsp;eV for Mn and 6.0&nbsp;eV for Ni.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0053_R_d122500884e1419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Phys. Rev. B">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The <i>E</i><sub>f</sub> was calculated by this equation

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202303812-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/da37a09b-7186-48f6-ab65-55a7165dccd5/adfm202303812-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202303812:adfm202303812-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202303812-math-0002.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mtable displaystyle="true">
                                    <mtr>
                                       <mtd columnalign="right">
                                          <mrow>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>E</mi>
                                                <mi mathvariant="normal">f</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>E</mi>
                                                <mi>total</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                             <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>E</mi>
                                                <mi>pre</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                             <msub>
                                                <mi>E</mi>
                                                <mi>unit</mi>
                                             </msub>
                                             <mo>/</mo>
                                             <mi>N</mi>
                                          </mrow>
                                       </mtd>
                                    </mtr>
                                 </mtable>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}{E}_{\rm{f}} = {E}_{{\rm{total}}}\ - {E}_{{\rm{pre}}} - {E}_{{\rm{unit}}}/N\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  
                  <p><i>E</i><sub>total</sub>: The total energy of layered oxide with heteroatoms.</p>
                  
                  <p><i>E</i><sub>pre</sub>: The energy of layered oxide without heteroatoms.</p>
                  
                  <p><i>E</i><sub>mon</sub>: The energy of heteroatoms unit cell.</p>
                  
                  <p><i>N</i>: The number of atoms within the unit cell.</p>
                  
                  <p>For the pre- and post-treatment of the calculation data, especially within dealing with different atoms’ PDOS data, support from vaspkit was drawn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0054_R_d122500884e1485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Comput. Phys. Commun.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And the Bader charge method was performed to analyze the electronic structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202303812-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202303812-bib-0055_R_d122500884e1490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
